Environmental Assessment
The project has been screened and given a Category SBE rating. Waste disposal sites may
have significant environmental impacts, thus a detailed enviromnental assessment was deemed
necessary for these sites. Preliminary environmental assessments, which were completed prior to
appraisal, confirmed that the selected sites would be suitable for proper sanitary landfill operations
without risks to the environment and that no displacement of persons would be involved in
establishing and developing the recommended sites. A more limited analysis has been made on the
remaining subprojects. A summary of these assessments follows.
Componet: RehabWlitation of 1Markes. LI&r Parks. Roads and Roadside Drains.
Positive Impacts
*     Provision of a safe water supply through additional standpipes and the upgrading of
sanitation facilities in the markets and lorry parks should improve health conditions in
the central town areas.
*     Improved solid waste and collection arrangements in areas of the city with higher
population densities will also improve health conditions, mainly by eliminating open
piles of refuse.
X     Improved circulation in an out of and within motor parks wilt reduce air pollution
from vehicle exhausts by decreasing traffic congestion.
*     Construction of roadside storm drains will eliminate ponding of stagnant water and
protect against localized erosion.
Negative Impacts
*     On-site sanitation, if not properly designed, may result in leaching of wastewater into
the ground water and lead to subsequent pollution problems.
*     The increase in sullage water from markets and storm water from drains, if not
properly managed, may create hazards.
Mitigation Measures
*     VIP latrines and aquaprivies to be constructed under the project will have pits that
will be periodically emptied, so that new pits do not have to be dug when the first
ones are fill.
*     Vacuum trucks will be provided in order that a timely and ,ffordable latrine emptying
service wil be available.



-59-
Anxn
Page 2 of 3
*     Education programs will be established to promote effective use of latrines.
*     Runoff from markets and storm water from roadside drains will be properly channeled
so as to avoid pollution or erosion.
Component - Solld and Liquid (human) Waste Management
Positive hnpacts
*     Ihe provision of new sanitary land fill sites and liquid (humati waste or septage)
disposal sites, and closure of existing sites which are preseny operated as open
dumping grounds with litfle control, will significantly reduce health problems and
envirounental degradation risks.
*     Engineering and environmental studies of new disposal sites have been undertaken to
identify means of minimizing potential environmental problems. Such means will be
incorporated in final designs and project implementation arrangements.
*     Exntending the refuse collection service to include unplanned housing areas will reduce
some of the environmental health problems associated with on-plot disposal.
-     The refuse collection process will become more hygienic, as there will be a reduction
in handling of refuse compared with present arrangements.
*     Adoption of more regular collection service will reduce the health risk attributable to
unattended refuse being left uncollected for up to one week and longer.
=     Encouragement of an improved latrine waste disposal program will contrbute to a
reduction in surface water pollution and associated envirommental health problems.
*     Establishment of a domestic latrine financing program, If successful, will reduce
pressure on public toilets and improve the overall rate of usage of sanitation facilities.
NLAtive Impacts
*     Higher concentrations of solid waste (at the new landfill site) and liquid waste may
contribute towards pollution of groundwater sources.
*     Potential smells and vectors from new landfIll disposal sites could present a nuisance.
Mitgton Proposals
*     Operational guidelines will be developed for use at the landfill sites which will be
enforced through proper supervision of operations.
*     Soils surveys have been undertaken into the suitability of ground conditions at the
sites and engineering measures to eliminate risk of groundwater pollution wil be a

*     Landscaping treatment of the landfill sites wIll help to screen sites from view.
*     The regular supervision of landfill sites wIll minimize the risk fom, and to,
scavenger.
*     Establishmt of dedicated maintenance funds will ensu  the proper operation and

developments on the residents' general willingness to pay' as they see improvements in the towns

ancial Trasfers by Central Government
22.   The existing financial transfers comprise the percentage grant for salaries and wages for the
main categories of DA staff; the ceded revenues; and some limited grants in aid of specific
development projects which often require DAs to commit to counterpart fumding. The policies relating
to transfers are presently being reviewed by government to improve both the amounts and objectives
of transfers and the reliability of fund flows to the DAs, to assist them in accepting and performing
transferred functions. The District Assembly Common Fund Act was approved by Parliament in
1993; however, the criteria and mechanisms for establishing the annual value of the fund and
distributing funds between the Districts have not yet been specified. For this to be effective a key
requirement will be a reliable and comprehensive DA Information System (DAIS). The Local
Government Support Unit (LGSU), approved for development in MLG under the Urban II project,
will be fiuther strengthened under this project and is expected to play an important role in the
speciftcation of the scope and operation of the DAIS.
23.   In determning fimre national budget allocations to the DAs it is considered essential that this
be set at a sustainable level; that is, a level which will not be significantly exposed to variations in
national budget performance. There are needs for two types of grant component: a) for support of the
recurrent budget, and b) to promote specific programs.
Policy Review of District Assemblies' Revenues
24.   Apart from the direct assistance to DAs in revenue mobilization and financial management,
die project will support the Government of Ghana in reviewing the current allocation of revenue
surces among Central Government and District Assemblies with the view to revising currently
established revenue systems consistent with established decenalization objectives. Technical
assistance will be provided to help GOG to: (a) conduct a comprehensive review of the current
allocation of revenue sources among central government and local governments, and (b) prepare draft
proposals for legad and regulatory reforms to rationalize revenue collection among levels of
government (consistent winth GOG's strategic dece ization objectives and the relative resource
requirements at both cental and district levels). This is explained in detail in Appendix 4 of this
Annex.
Revenue MobiflztIon
25. Revenue mobilization is one of the keystones of urban sector development in Ghana, and
ADRP and Urban II bothf focussed on improvements in property taxation and specific fees and
ciarges to increa  the financial capabilities of the Metropolitan and Municipal Assemblies. This wil
be continued through this project. Detaied Revenue Improvement Action Plans (RIAP) will be
prepared for each DA. An oudine of the RJAP is attached as Appendix 2 of this Annex. The RIAP
identifies: al the major existg revenues and the specific key actions required to be taken by the DAs
also any other involved agencies (eg. the Land Valuation Board); b) any new revenues identified as
having potential for specific DAs; c) revenue administation and collection policies; d) the public
information campaigns considered necessary to inform the public of how revenues collected are used
in the delivery of public services; and e) the identification and deletion of charges which are not cost



-65-
Annex
Page S of 7
effective. In addition, the RIAP will identify an action timetable and develop key monitoring
Indicators so that RIAP progress can be effectively managed by the DAs. Outline RIAPs were
prepared by consultants prior to Appraisal. The final detailed RIAPs will be prepared under an
additional consulmancy, the TOR for which is attached as Appendix 3. The DAs' acceptance of and
commitment to the achievement of RIAPs Is considered an essential element of the project, and their
formal ratification by the DAs will be a condition of Credit Effectiveness.
Basic Rate
26.   The Basic Rate is a poll tax (ocally called a sleeping tax') which has historically contributed
between 2% and 10% of DA revenues. It is declining in real terms in the majority of DAs for the
following reasons: a) it is reportedly resented by the town residents; b) it is a low unit value tax
(C200 per capita) and major collection efforts are not considered justified; and c) there is a history of
Tleakages- of collections.
27. The financing of the costs of DA activities through both a Poll Tax and the Property Tax also
has elements of duplication in terms of the same tax payers in some cases being required to pay both.
It may be dtat consideration should be given to elimination of the poll tax. This proposal was put
forward by the DAs in the workshop held at the commencement of the pre-appraisal mission in March
1993.
Property Rates
28. The historical performance of property rates has varied, ranging from 3% to 20% of DA
revenue. This variation reflects improving trends in some DAs and declining trends in others.
Collections relate primaily to private sector properties. State and parastata institions have not
paid; there is no payment in lieu (PIL) in respect of govenment premies. Action has been taken by
MLGRD, and instructions to DAs to charge the state and parastatal institutions were issued in
September 1992 and Gazetted In October 1992. This should significantly improve the financial
capabilities of a number of DAs. The question of PIL has still to be resolved. The instructions
indicate, however, that the level of rate impost for establishments covered will be notified by central
government.
29.  ITe most important constait on property rate mobilization is the lack of current valuation
rolls. Origina valuation rolls were prepared in the 1960s, and there have been limited and infrequent
supplementary rolls issued since that dme. At present valuation rolls are not up to date in terms of
either the properties or the ratable values. Some of the DAs have attempted to compensate for this
through the application of a form of differential rating, but this is obviously only 'stop gap situation.
30. There is a clear need for the preparation and maintenance of current property valuation rolls
in the eleven DAs. Provisions have been made to extend the LVB valuation project operating under
Urban n to the eeven DAs on completion of the Urban II cities, which is expected prior to LGDP
effectiveness in mid 1994. hIital LVB estmates indicate that the cost of updating valuation rolls for
the deven towns Is on the order of US$0.68 million; thi is included in the project costs.
31. A key element in rate collections is the billing and payment recording systems. The present
sym   are slow ad inefficient, and there Is a need for dwelopment of improved systems, possibly
computerid, to establish prompt billing systems and payment recording systems which also generate
infoma    for rate wears follow-up and recovery action. Tecnical assistance (detailed in Annae



-66-
Annex
Page 6 of 7
15, Section 2) will help the District Assemblies to develop improved systems, including computerized
systems where applicable, The improved computer systems will be designed to accept basic
computerized property data from LVB to produce rate bills and rate ledgers, to receive payment
data, and to produce arrears notices for further collection efforts.
32.   There is also a clear need for education and public awareness campaigns relating to the annual
budgeting and rate fixing carried out by the DAs. The MLGRD instructions regarding rate charges to
government agencies of 29th September 1992 clearly states the role of property taxes as the residual
DA financing mechanism for all net budgeted expenditure (capital and recurrent) after allowing for
budgeted local revenues and government grants, and taking into account rates chargeable on
parastatals and government properties.
Market Tolls and Lorry Park Tolls
33.   The analysis and projections in appendices 1.1 - 1.11 indicate the current importance of
market and lorry park tolls as the major revenue source of the DAs. The two revenues are interlinked
and are subject to the same revenue characteristics. This project proposes rehabilitation works which
wfll significantly improve market and lorry park infrastructure and which should improve revenues as
a result. RAP actions have been identified to ensure that these revenues are maximized. Current
privatization considerations for market operation and revenue collection should ensure that the DA net
revenue position adequately reflects the improvements made and true revenue potential of the markets.
Potential New Revenues
34.   Potential new revenues fall into two categories: a) expansion of revenue bases through the
provision of services for which charges are already levied, ic. solid waste disposal and sanitation; and
b) completely new revenues. The major areas of new sustainable revenue sources relate to the
possible introduction of turnover related taxes, ie. for hotels and restaurants, entertainment
establishments (to the extent that is not already covered by the existing entertainments duty), etc. Tbis
will be considered ftrough the RIAP. Other sources of new revenue identfied by the DAs relate to
one time gains through specific projects or investments.
Revenue Management
35.   Gains through improved revenue management are expected to arise from the adoption of more
cost-effective collection policies; the use of collection incentive schemes; setting and annual review of
collection targets; minimizing, if not eliminating revenue leakages; the efficient use of bonded revenue
collection agents; and institution of efficient arrears control, reporting and recovery procedures. RIAP
proposals are expected to extend to recommended actions in these areas.
Accountng and Budgeting
36.   In preparing the historical financial analysis of DA financial performance, the account&lg,
budgeting and reporting procedures and structures were reviewed. There are significant weaknesses in
terms of the limited range of budget allocation heads. nTis has resulted in lack of any information
with regard to cost recovery services. In addition to the limited range of analysis, the DAs make no
allocaton of personnel costs. This is a nationa problem. 'Te accounting regulations covering the
DAs were prepared in 1961, and have not been modified since that time. The situation is curently
under review in both MLGRD and MFEP.



-67-
Page 7 of 7
37.   There is a requirement for a form of activity based budgeting which Identifies specific
operational activitias separately from the central support functions (eg. the District Executive and
Finance Officer's activities). The differing scale and complexities of the 110 Assemblies require a
system which can be operated on both a manual and computer basis (dependent on size and
capability). The Urban II project Is undertaking the development of a computerized system for the
five major Assemblies. It is expected that this system will be relevant and implementable in some of
the Assemblies in this project, possibly with limited amendment. However, there is an additional
requirement for an equivalent manual system for smaller Assemblies. Separate TA has been provided
for this task which Is described in Annex 15.
38.   Ihere are currently no instructions regarding the preparation of multi year financial plans.
This is considered a major omission as there is no longer term plan or framework for DA decision
taking. It is recommended that the MLGRD LGSU review this and make recommendations to
MLGRD) for decision.
39.   Annex 3 addresses the issue of establishing a separate fund for O&M of infrastructure
provided through this project; the methodology of account operation will be that World Bank funds
will be transferred to this account each year, after DA funds have been deposited, and contract
maintenance payments will be made from the account after proper certification. Provisions should be
a subject for consolidation in future accounting and budgeting improvement proposals.
Technical Assistance
40.   Anmex 5, Section 2 describes technical assistance to be provided, through national consultants,
for improving budgeting and accounting systems, implementing RIAPs, and upgrading billing and
collection of property rates. A total of 160 person-months of national consultants will be provided to
the 11 DAs over a three year period.

REPUBLIC OF GHANA
LOCAL GOVERNMET DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Terms of Refetence for Preparation of Revenue Improvement Adion
Plans for District AssenbUes
Background
1.    The GOO is proposing tD seek a crdit from the World Bank for the Local Government
Development Project which is itded to assist and strengthen the District Assemblies. Ihe project
would include a maximum of 11 towns and would be prepared following standard project preparation
in ta cmponent woud be sdected, prepared and costed in detail. Ihe Projed is to provide
assistance to District Assemblies:
(a)   to Improve esnta Ifastucre and services, and
(b)   to strengthen District Assemblies' planning, financial and managerial capabilities.
'the towns in the project are:
Bolgatanga          Bawku
Cape Coast          Elmina
Ho                  Keta
Koforda             Sunyan
Swedru              Techiman
Wa
2.    As a part of the strn hening of District Assemblies' planning, financial and management
capability it is proposed to improve local financial resource mobilization by the development and
Implementation of Revue Improvement Action Plans (RIAP) for each of the Distict Assemblies.
Oudine RIAP have been prepared by the original project preparation consultancy; they now require
completion so tat each District Assembly may review and approve the detailed RIAP prior to
fiization of the project cedit.
3.    Mhe Misty of Local Government and Rural Development  eirs approximaty 10 staff
months of cosultancy services to carry out the scope of work and tasks below, in the period
September 1993 to December 1993.
Scope of Work and Ta1k
(a)    Review oudine RLAP from original consultancy with each District Assembly and
ensre tat ondy major revenues are included.
(b)   lidetfy the detailed acons and steps required to improve each of the revenues, and
aes the impact, in financial terms, of the proposed actions.



-86-                              An=  5
Appendix 3
Page 2 of 2
(c)   Identify, with each Distrct Assembly, the other agencies involved In the actions
required, and meet jointly with all agencies to establish a realistic timetable for
achievement of the RIAP actions and benefits.
(d)   Identify critical monitoring indicators of progress towards achievement of RIAP
benefits and prepare a detailed RLAP for each District Assembly.
Reports
4.    A detailed RlAP for each of the 11 District Assemblies (in 6 copies) together with an overall
final report of findings in relation to District Assembly revenues should be submitted to Ministry of
Local Government and Rural Development by 31st December 1993.

REPUBLIC OF GHANA
LOCAL GOVERNMENT DEVELOPMENT PROJECr
Strengthening lhe Revenue Capadty of District Assemblies
Policy Review
Purpose
1.    The purpose of this component is to assist the Government of Ghana to review the current
allocation of revenue sources among Central Govermnent and District Assemblies with the view tO
revising currently established revenue systems consistent with established decentralization objectives.
Background
2.    District Assemblies are almost completely dependent on Central Government for both
investment and recurrent finance. As presented in the Bank's recent study, "GHANA -
Strengthening Local Initiatve and Building Local Capacity-, the most fundamental problem affecting
the current attempt to implement Ghnas decentralization policy is the inconsistency between the
devolutionary objectives of PNDC Law No. 207 and the structure of authority in place for the
performance of planning, financing, and personnel management functions. As stressed throughout the
referenced study, the system in place is largely one in which District Assemblies act as agents of
Central Government rather than exercising significant discretionary authority as envisaged in PNDC
Law No. 207 and the new Constitution. Significant improvement of public sector pertormae in
Ghana is not possible until that inconsistency is resolved. Resolving that asymmetry between systems
in place and the Government's stated policy objective must be the cornerstne of its strategy for
implementing decentralization.
3.    The GOG is fully aware that the current situation contradicts, to some extent, its own
decentralization policy objectives and is drafting changes in its financial management systems which
are expected to be more consistent with those objectives. This sub-component of the LGDP will
assist Government to rationalize its longer-tm policy with respect to the allocation of revenue
sources among central government and District Assemblies.
Desciption
4.    Comprehensive assistance for the Implementation of Government's evolving decentralization
program is beyond the scope of the LGDP. Nevertheless, because of its imporace for susiing the
specific benefits of the LGDP, the project will assist Government to rationalize its system for
generating and sharing public sector revenue among central goverlment and District Assemblies.
Although emphasis will be placed on priority actions most directly required for sustaning the benefits
of LGLP, this activity wfll also contribute significantly to improved public sector performance at
local levels in most sectors supported by the Bank in Ghana.
5.    Technical assistance wiUl be provided to support GOG in: (a) conducting a comprehensive
review of the current allocation of revenue sources among centa goverment and local govnents
and (b) preparing draft proposals for legal and regulatory reforms to rationalize revenue collection



-58- ~~            ~       A~
Appeadiz 4
Page  of 3
among levels of government (consistent with GOO' s ategic decentraization objectives and the
relative resource requirements at both central and district Ieels). Figue I provides an outline of
actvities, responsibe parties, and the time reired to complet planned taskL In summay, ths
Fmance Committee of Parlment and a speciized Working Group would be assisted to: (a) design,
organize, and conduct Action-Planning Workshops to idetif scific issues; (b) idetfy, desrb,
and assess alteraive formulae and systm for the distibution of revene ources among central
government and District Assemblies; and (c) adopt and eablish apropriate policies and systems.
Technical assistce, training, operational support, and computer hardware and relevat software
would be financed in support of that process. Dets of the TA inputs ars presented in Annex IS,
Section S. Twelve person-mondhs of national consultants will be provided, plus fiacing for
workshops, a computer, and logistical support. Total base cost is US$.056 million.

Summar
Qbiive: To provide a comprehensive register of rateable properties in each of the 11 LGDP
project towns to enhance existing revenue from this source as part of a package of measures designed
to Improve revenue mobilization and financial management In each of the DAs.
Descrptio: Technical assistance, vehicles, other equipment, training and operational support for
property revatuation In each of the project towns to be undertaken by the LVB. This component will
be closely associated with the related strengthening of budgeting, reverue collection and accounting
systems in aU the DAs.
oation: Cape Coast, Elmina, Sunyani, Koforidua, Ho, Bolgatanga, Bawku, Techinn, Swedru,

Detailed Descipton
1.    The Jroject will provide for a revaluation of all rateable properties to be carried out in each
of the project towns. The LVB has experience of successfblly completing revaluation exercises in
Accra and Tema under ADRP and is currently involved in similar work under Urban II in Kumasi,
Tamale and Sekondi-Takoradi. LVB's work under Urban HI is expected to be completed by March
1994 which would enable It to commence work under LGDP in mid 1994. LVB has developed the
necessar expertise for effective implementation and supervision of revaluation work and the project
will further assist it to build its capacity to maintain the new Lists and to carry out similar work in
other Districts in due course. It is important that LVB retain sufficient capacity to maintain the Lists
produced under previous projects if the benefit of that work is to be fully realized.
2. Estimates of the number of properties to be surveyed and assessed In each of the project
towns, based on the existing numbers of properties contained in LVB records, are as follows:

and supervision through a streamlined management structure.
4.    LVB has sufficient existing staff resources to undertake the work but will need support under
the project in the form of vehicles, survey and other fieldwork equipment, office equipment and
materials, and additional computer facilities to supplement and update its existing computers which
are limited in terms of their application and potential. LVB will need technical assistance in the
selection of appropriate computer facilities and in the design of necessary programs. New software
and hardware for LVB should be designed to be compatible with computer facilities to be supplied to
DAs under the project. This will enable data transfer (via disk or tape) between LVB and the DAs
relating to the production and maintenance of the Valuation Lists to be implemented in a more
efficient and cost-effective manner. New computer facilities to be made available to MILGRD/LGSU
should also be able to accept data from LVB/DAs to assist in effec:ive monitoring operations.
5.    The design of computer software for the DAs should contain the fcility for automated
production of tate demands to improve billing and collection operations. Appropriate hardware will
also be required for this purpose. Software to be provided for use by the LVB should be designed to
enable survey information to be held in a computer database and should incorporate a program that
would facilitate the application of replacement cost scales to survey data so that the computer can
produce draft rateable values automatically. The program should also facilitate the application of an
appropriate index to update assessments in the years between revaluation exercises.
6.    The project will provide for training of LVB staff, mainly locally, to enhmce their
management capacity and to assist in the effective maintenance of the new Valuation Lists produced.
Training will also be needed to fully utilize the new computer facilities provided under the project.
As a separate but related element, DA staff wil also receive chWnical assistance and training to
enable them to maximize the reenue potential that will be available from the updated, comprehensive
tax base resulting from the revaluation of rateable properties. The project will provide operational
suWort to LVB to assist them in meeting the additional expenditure and other needs dtat wil arise as
a result of the revaluation exercises.

REPUBLC OF GHANA
LOCAL GOVERNMNT DEVELOPMET PROJECr
Land Use Pbnning and Developinent Guidance
Objecives
1.    This component of the LGDP will support a collaborative effort between the Town and
Countrv Planning Department and the 11 District Assemblies to strengthen the system for managing
land use planning and development guidance in the project towns.
Backgound
2.    The primary goal of land use plaming and development guidance in the context of the 11
LODP towns is to guide public and private investment in infrasructure and building construction in
such a way as to produce (1) efficient urban growth and (ii) reduced environmental problems.
Sustainable land use planning measures are needed to identify and preserve minimum land
reservations for lnfrastructure and public services, which compete poorly for space in the free land
market. In addition, new approaches are needed to obtain the voluntary cooperation of land owners
in achieving land uee planning objectives.
3.    In the present cimstances, traditional development controls (i.e. zoning, subdivision
regulations, and building pennits) function poorly due to several factors. It is very difficult for
reserved infrastrOcture corridors and or service areas to be acquired outright by the government due
to lack of financial resources and weaknesses in land expropriation procedures. In the absence of
government's acquiring property rights over the reservadons, these are highly exposed to
enroachment by land speulators and private developers long before public agencieb are able to
mobilize resources to develop them for their intended public purposes. The subdivision of land
parcels into lots for subsequent sale Is a common practice, but the ability to control or guide it is
severtly restricted by weak institutional capacity. The current building permit process is excessively
complex and, in practice, its implementation is ineffective. Other problems of the planning and
development control system include: lack of up-to-date maps, inadequate logistical resources for
planning staff and a lack of understanding of the purposes of land use planning on the part of most
government officials and staff.
Desciption
4.    LGDP wil assist wfith a limited program of support focusing on development pian  (as
disict from contol). Ihis will comist of the following elements:
a)    Production of maps of the towns.
b)    Rapid preparation of simple town-wide land use plans indicating (1) primaryr areas of
fuure uran growth over the coming 10-15 years, (ii) areas where development
should be avoided for environmental reasons, (iii) designation of major new roa'i
coridors in peripheral grcwth areas, and (iv) designation of selected public servte
areas.

Page 2 of 3
c)    ampemeng ground demarcaon of major road dght-of-way as indicated on the
lani e plans, folowing condsultation and cooperaion of the inlved land owners.
This will be done to eu  the vilitq and prtecton from encroachment of the
cotridors.
d)    Preparation of simple guidelines for subdivisions. ocal TCP staf will be trained in
using these guidelines to provide assistance to land owners who seek advice. SiWple
manuas on land subdivision, suitable for non-speciists, will be prepared and made
available to intest  land owners.
e)    Strengthening record-keeping systems related to development control.
f)    Education and awareness campaigns to foster coopera    of prvate land owners and
public agencies.

training. It will also finace vehicles and other equipment for the six Regional offices of the Town

REPUBIUC OF GHANA
LOCAL GOVERNMENT DEVEOPMENT PROJECT
mapping Component
Objectves
1.    An important objecdve of LGDP Is to Improve the availability of information for urban
plannig, infrstructure programming, and revenue mobilization by District Authorities (DAs). Ihe
Government and the World Bank have agreed that up to date base maps are an Important component
of this information base. Such maps are essential to enable land use planners, engineers, land valuers,
and providers of utility services to carry out their work properly. IDA will support the Survey
Department in the production of new base maps for the eleven towns in the LGDP.
Badkground
2.    For the last 20 years almost no  re scale mapping has been produced in Ghana. The urban
mapping produced in the 1950s, 60s and early 70s has not been updated nor extended to cover areas
of recent urbanization. In consequence the original investment in urban mapping is now an asset of
very limited value and must be replaced by new mapping. Surprisingly large quantities of some
sheets (several hundred copies every few months) are still being issued by the Survey Department,
when they are able to purchase regular supplies of printing paper to supply the demand.
3.    Lack of large scale mapping is causing massive duplication of effort Ihe Land Valuation
Board has had to produce its own sketch maps of Accra for the Urban II valuation component. Ghana
Watr and Sewerage Corporation has recely commissioned French conutants to produce an
inventy of water consumers in Greater Accra to improve revenue collection, that includes producing
simple maps of all connections to consumers. Town & Country Planning has extended and updated
old maps from aeria photography and field inspection, but recognizes that their information on the
current stas of urban development is outdated and inadequate. The District Authority in Keta is
using hand traced copies of very old Survey Department maps. Surveys for engineering design and
maintece for roads, storm draiage, and wat and electricity distibution networks all have to be
commissioned individually because of the lack of base maps. If the Survey Department were able to
produce up-to-date maps covering the full extent of current urban development and surrounding land
subject to development, tis duplication of effort would be avoided, and all users would have
compatible and reliable maps. Government policies are formulated and adninistered on inaccurate
and incomplete information which must reduce thei relevance and effectiveness. Ghana Water and
Sewerage Corporation loses a very substantW  proportion of its potential revenue because of the lack
of records of consumers which would be easy to rectify if up-to-date large scale urban mapping were
available. GWSC is aware that mapping would help to increase their revenue and have intimated their
willingness to contribute to the cost.
4.    The nitial cost of mapping would be recovered by cost avoidance in duplicated effort and
greater efficiency by users over a reasonable period.



-98-
Page 2 of 6
Description
S.    The requirements are to fly aerial photography, establish ground control, produce digital
mapping and distribute maps and other survey information to the end users. The work is to be
executed by the Ghana Survey Department supported by an internati3nal Consultant. The work may
be divided int the following tasks.
Prenmrking on the ground
6.    The Survey Deparment shall premark existing survey monuments in each town by painting
them prior to photography. ITis reduces the amount of survey work to be done after photograhy.
Aerial photography
7.    The Consultant shall plan and fly aerial photography of the eleven towns, process the films
and produce prints, diapositives, index plots, and flight reports.
Grond control
8.    The Survey Department (or licensed surveyors) shall establish the necessary ground control
for mapping by premarking existing control points prior to photography and photo-point
identification after photography as appropriate. The pattern of plan and height control points
necessary for aerial triangulation and to pfovidW sufficient redundancy to isolate errors shall be agreed
between the Survey Department and the Consultant.
Aerial tr   aon
9.    The Consultant shall observe and adjust the aerial triangulation of each town as a single block
using a recognized and proven package for block or bundl3 adjustment. The Consultant shall notify
the Survey Department of any errors detected in the ground control results and the Survey
Departuent shall check and confirm the correct coordinates and photo-identifications.
Digital mapping at 12500 scale
10.   The Consultant shall produce samples and preliminary plots of the digital mapping at 1:2500
scale of the towns listed in the Bill of Quantities. (One town may be withdrawn to be mapped by the
Survey Department).
Field compledon
11.   The Survey Department, or local licensed surveyors, shall carry out field completion on the
ground to collect names and other information not visible on the aerial photographs.
EdtiUn and delivery of final maps and digital data
12.   The Consultant shall edit all the required corrections and additions and supply the digital data
and final map sheettarecies.



-99-                               Annei
Page 3 of 6
Dtbution of hardcopy amps and digital data
13.   'he Survey Departnent hall be responsible for the distribution of hardcopy maps and digital
data to the end users. This shall include publicizing the products available to both national and local
government agencies and the public. A realistic price shall be charged to all users to cover the real
cost of retrieval and reproduction with some contribution to the cost of the mapping program.
Mainenane of mapping
14.   The Survey Department shall make arrangements to maintain the currency of the maps by
undertaking periodic revision surveys and issuing revised editions of the maps. A resident
professional surveyor should be posted to each of the eleven towns to provide advice and undertake
map revision, settng out of development layouts, land title surveys etc.
Techimcal Assistance
15.   The Consultant will supply the following technical assistance:
a)    A project manager/surveyor for four months, intermittendy, to assist the Survey
Departnent in the following tasks:
-     Planning and management of all aspects of the project;
-     Preparing mondtly reports on progress against program and any technical,
organizational or contractual difficulties arising and recommendations for
improvement;
-     Defining the requirements for photocontrol and assisting the Survey
Department to check the quality of the results;
-     Assisting the Survey department with field completion and ensuring that the
corrections and additions are clearly understood by the Consultant;
-     Discussing and resolving final corrections and delivery of final maps.
b)    An experienced reprographics specialist familiar with large scale map reproduction for
two weeks to identify the requirements for fast and economical reproduction of large
scale maps for Urban m, to recommend suitable equipment and consumables, and to
prepare specifications for procurement.
c)    An experienced photogrammetric supervisor/operator for four months to work in the
Photogrammetric Section of the Survey Department to train s"" 'dvise ci the
introduction of digital mapping techniques, demonstrate tht .-..dards of quality and
producdvity that should be achieved, and make recommendations for fiutre
Inmprovements.
Equipment
16.   'he Survey Department requires the following equipment to produce and handle digital
mapping and to print maps for distribution to users.
a)    Digital mapping system: Ihe Survey Department has 24 photogrammetric operators
(trained at ITC in the Netherlands or Ile-Ife in Nigeria) but no photogrammetric

instruments for digital mapping. One photogranmetric analytical plotter, PC based
interactive digial mapping system, Al sizo plotter and appopriate software will bo
purchased to enable the Survey Department lo map one of the eleven towns and then
to start a program of digital mapping from the photooraphy flown for Urban IIb.is
would reduce overstaffing of the photogmmetric instrumens already installed
(mainly very old and needing major renovation or scrapping), and these insme
can be used for corventional mamn  mapping. An experienced photogrammetric
supervisor/operator shoud be supplied by the Consultant for 4 months to assist in the
Ltroducton of digital mapping, and setting realistic standards of quWality and

b)    Reprographics equipment: Ibe printing equipment suitable for printing large scale
maps is al more than 30 years old, very unreliable or unusable, and Inefficient for
fast and economical production of large scale maps on demand. The existing 1:2500
scale maps are uneconomically small and it has been agreed to adopt a metric sheet
size covering 2km x lkm for which new printing equipment is essental. The Survey
Department wfll seek the advice of a specialist in cartographic reproduction to ideatify
the requirements, prepare specifications for the procurement of equipment and
consumables to keep the map users in the eleven towns supplied with single color
maps of an acceptable quality at affbrdable cost.
Standards for Urban Mapping
17.   MU Sale: 1:2500 scale is a realistic scale to adopt for general urban mapping, provided it is
produced to a consistendy high standard of accuracy, content and presentation with rigorous quality
management. It is the traditional scale that has been used for many years in Ghana and is well
understood by map users. With digital mapping, it will be possiblh to enlarge town centres to 1:1250
scale where ftis is neceary for parikular applications, even though this wil not increase the
accuracy or content of the map.
18.   Tye of ma product: Digital line maps are printed maps very similar to the conventional
line maps, but can be displayed on computer screens, printed selectvely at different scales, and used
to link and analyze all kinds of spatially related information held in computer databases. All the
inteaonal consultants that might be contcted to produce urban mapping would supply digit data
as a by-product. The long term poteni  for users to add value to the map data by merging them
with other information justifies adopting digit mapping at the commencement of the urban mapping
regeneration phase.
19. Accurm: The accuracy of plotted detail should match the users' ability to scale points and
distances from paper copies which is likely to be the standard medium for the foreseeable future.
Higher accuracies can be achieved by digital mapping and may well be attained in practice but should
not be specified contractually as this may increase the costs without matial benefit to the users.
20.   The stndard accuracy for map detail may be stated as follows:
a)    Grid lines and control points shall be drawn to an accuracy better than -+ 0.3 mm
maximum tolerance.



-101-                            mz
Annex 8
Page S Of 6
Well-defined points of det shall be plotted In their true positions at map scale to
better than i 0.3 mm root mean squre error, when coordinates are scaled off the
map from the nearest grid lines and compared with coordinates determined by prcise
measurement on the ground from the neart cntrol point. (90% of a representatve
sample of well-defined points shall be within + 0.5 mm.)
b)    Similarly the accracy of height information must be related to the needs of generad
users. Detailed engineering works may require higher accuracy in paricular locations
and must be commissioned for the purpose. The generally adopted standards for
contours and heights are as foilows:
c)    Contours shall be correct to a root mean square eror of better than one third of the
contour itval where a representaive sample of points on contour lines is checked
by precise measurement from the nearest control point. (90% of a representative
sample shall be correct to better than half the specifled contour intera.)
d)    Spot heights shall be correct to a root mean square error of better than E 0.3 metres
where a representaive sample is checked by precise measurement from the nearest
control point. (90% of a representve sample shaU be corect to better than + 0.5
metres.)
21. NMaQM: Detil that is usefiul to the majority of users and can be obtained by direct
capture from the aerial photography should be plotted as the marginal cost of extra information is low
pwvided it does not clutter tle map and confuse the general user. Some additional information, not
visible on the aerial photography, such as names of streets and important buildings and road edges
obscured by trees, most be collected on the ground. Ihis is a time consuming and relatively costly
task which should be kept to a minimum.
22.   The old 1:2500 scale line maps have correcdy identified the information normally shown at
this scale, and users are famliar with this. The Town & Country Planning Department have
expressed interest in recordicg the number of storeys (floors) of buildings with more than a single
storey. As this informaion can be extracted direct from the photographs with reasonable certainty at
marginal additional cost, it is recommended that this should be done without being confirmned by
inspecdon on the ground.
23.   Mericaion: It has been Government policy since 1974 to convert all mapping to metric
units. As the urbsm mapping of eleven towns wil be new mapping unconnected to existing maps at
this scale, it provides the ideal opporunity to implement this policy.
24.  Tne Ghana National Grid on a local transverse mercator projtdon in imperial feet, has
already been recalculated In metres and should be used for all future urban mapping. Coordinates
are also available on the Universal Transverse Mercaor Projection which is valuable for international
mapping and exchange, but is not suitable for national mapping as it introduces unnecessarily  ge
scale factors and convergence. Height infoRnnation currently available in feet can easily be converWt
to metres and new contours drawn at vertical intervals of 2 metres.
25.      H     d _   The old 1:2500 scale maps showed 5 foot contours but no spot heights.
In dense urban areas, contus often conflict with planimetric detail and are usually suppressed.
Contours dran at the slighlly wider interal of 2 metres supplemented by spot heights along roads,



-102-
Page 6 of 6
a high and low points and in flat open spaces will provide the uers with more height data without
cluttering the map,
26.   lhe contours and spot height should be recorded as thdimensional data suitable for digital
terrain modelling when the requirement arises.

Detailed Description
InsttutIonal Stregtheng and Capacity-Building
ObJectives
1.    The objectives of the Institutional Strengthing and Capacity -Building component of LGDP are:
(a) To strengthen the capacity of 11 District Assemblies to plan and manage their available financial,
human, and material resources in an integrated and sustainable manner and (b) To strengthen ceal
govement's capacity to support the activities of the 11 Project District Assemblies consistent with
GOG's overall dentrization policy.
Loation
2.    Within the 11 towns of Bawku, Bolgatanga, Cape Coast, Elmina, Ho, Keta/Anloga,
Koforidua, Sunyani, Swedru, Techiman, and Wa as well as MLGRD's Local Govenment Support
Unit (LGSU), Trainig Support Unit (TSIJ), and Town and County Planming Deparment (TCPD);
also the Survey Department (SD), Land Valuaion Board (LVB), and Tecdnical Services Cete (TSC)
in Accra.
Background
3.    The LGDP is the fourth in a series of IDA-financed urban infrastrucre Improvement projects
in Ghana. The three previous IDA-financed projects (ADRP, PWP, and Urban II) have ontributed
to, and/or are continuing, improvement of MLGRD's capacity to provide effcive support to local
govenments in the context of GO's current decentralization policy. Under the Urban I Project,
MLGRD is being assisted to establish the LGSU, to assist, coordinate, and monitor District
Assemblies with respect to their roles in Implementg urban irastructure projects, and the TSU,
which wil be responsible for designing and implementg a local government training program.
Nevertheless, the LODP is the first Bank-supported urban development project to be fully designed
and implemented following the most recett articulation of GOG's decentralization policy.
4.    The ititudonal strengthening and capacity-building effrts financed by LGDP take into
account GOG's evolving decenization policy. I/ As reflected in a growing body of legislation,
2I the continuing premise of Ghana's decentralization policy is that District Assemblies will

sree; (v) markets; (vi) car parks; and (vii) waste management system.
5.    District Assemblies, as corporate bodies, perform both legiWative and executive functions.
Lesative authority resides in a body of Counclors, two-thirds of whom ar popularly elected. The
legislative body of the Assembly must meet at least four times a year. Executive authority resides In
an Executive Committee. lbat Committee consists of not more than one-third of the total number of
councilors elected from among themselves by the total membership of the Distiict Assembly. 3/ A
District Chief Executive, appoinbd by the President with the prior approval of two-thirds of the
members of the District Assembly (present and voting), serves as Chairman of the District Executive
Committe. It is the District Executive Committee wbich implements th policies of the Assembly.
Five Sub-Committees of the District Executive Committee are mandated by law: (i) Development
Planning; (ii) Social Services; (iii) Works; (iv) Justice and Security; and (v) Finance and
Administration. Additional Sub-Commitees can be established by District Assemblies at their own
discretion. Untl now, the entities responsible for actually implementing the legiated policies of the
Assembly and executive decisions of ^he District Executive Committee (and Sub-Committees) have
been the staff of the 22 former central government departments assigned to district levels. Those staff
were made nominally responsible directy to District Assemblies by PNDC Law NO. 207 in 1988; but
that intention has not yet ben effectively Implemented.
6.    Although new and projected legislation represents progress towards significant autonomy of
District Assemblies, primary reliance on central government for investment finance and staff will
remain for the indefinite flture. Nevertheless, specific actions can be taken by District Assemblies,
within the scope of exisig legislation, to increase their own-generated revenue and exercise
managerial discredon with respect to staff deployment. The LGDP responds to those pportnimes by
strngthening the capacity of the 11 District Assemblies to more effectively use the discetionary
power avalable to them.

8.    ImiM at                Caggeit   -  di        Asmbli. The current authority of District
Assemblies was recendy enhanced by the enactment of the nev, Civil Service Law, 1993, which
established an Office of District Administration responsible to a District Coordinat   Officer (DCO).
Each DCO is responsible for co-ordinating the activities of District Assembly Departments; this is a
new designation for the previous 22 central government departments which were nominally
decentralized in 1988. Further enhancement of the authority of District Assemblies is expected
through a new Local Govemnment Act (1993) which has been drafted and will soon be submitted to
Parliament. This new Act, if approved, will reinforce the intentions of the current PNDC Law No.
207 by providing more detail with respect to important implememation requirements.
9.    The legislative evolution of decentralization has not, however, been matched by equivalent
implementation. Past experience with decentralization policy in Ghana suggests the need for an
explicit implementation program to give practical effect to such policies. District Assemblies can, and
must, do more to exercise the authority already provided to fully integrate and cohesively deploy the
staff of the 22 former departments at district level. Tle project supports District management in four
ways: (a) district reorganization; O) improved local policy and legislative oversight; (c) management
team-building; and (d) pilot use of personal services contracts.
10.   District Reorganizton. Although inadequate skills among staff assigned to District
Assemblies contribute to lack of capacity at local levels, additional problems arise from: ()
ambiguous authority relationships among Assemblymen/women, executive staff, and the staff of
previously centralized departments assigned to local levels and Cii) insufficient attention to assigning
apprpriate persons to required tasks and integrang approprie personnel into effective action-teams.
Therefore, the immediate objective of this sub-component is improved capacity to implement and
sustain project befits trough effective integration of the staff of previously centralized departments
assigned to district level. LGDP supports that objective by assisting the project's 11 District
Assemblies to reduce the number of dstrict-level departments and establish an effectve intd
structure within departments. To complement this internal reorganization of executive functions, the
orgaation of legislative responsibilities for urban development policy-decisions and oversight will
also be reinforced within the Assemblies themselves.
11.   The cument draft Local Govermment Act, 1993 will amend previous legislation by
distinguishng among three different types of districts: (i) Metropolis; 0i) Municipality; and (iii)
Distict. The primary factors distinguishing one type from another will be population size, density,
and type of economic activity. All 11 LGDP District Assemblies would be included in the District
category; i.e., the least urbanized. District Assemblies in the least urbanized category will be
required to establish 11 District Department 41 which are meant to replace the previous structure of
the 22 decentralized central government departments at the district level. However, although the
departmental structure of District Assemblies will be mandated by the new legislation, the intemal
strucre and staffing of those 11 departments will be at the discretion of each District Assembly. For
example, although a Department of Agriculture and Forestry is mandated, District Assemblies will
retn complete discretion as to whether or not their Department would contain a Fisheries Unit and,
MmTh 11 madated District Dpartments which t least urband District Assemblies will be rquired to
saubHsh are.: (i) Centa Ad a ;  ) Fimnce; (ii) Educatioa and Sports; (iv) Distict Haet,;
(v) Agicultu  and Forestry; (vi) Plnnig; (vii) Soca Wefr and Community Developmt (vi)
Natural Rusour  Consration; (ix) Work; (x) Industry and Trade; and (xi) Disa  Prevento

if so, how that unit would be staffed. Indeed, a District Assembly will be legally authorized to staff
only that part of a Department which focusses on its priority programs.
12.   LGDP will assist each of the 11 District Assemblies to formulate and implement a
reorganiztion plan tailored to its own priority programs. Specifically, the District Co-ordinating
Officer will be assisted by the District Management Consultants (see Annex 15, Section 3) financed
by the project. Emphasis will be placed In the first instance on the organization and integration of
those functions most Important for the implementation and sustainability of the infrastructure
investments financed by LGDP.
13.   Policy and Legislative Oversight. The Works and Finance and Administration Sub-
Committees of the District Executive Committee will be assisted to improve the efficiency and
effectiveness of their oversight function with respect to the relevant LGDP component by including
them directly in the policy formulation and Implementation activities of the Project. The Works Sub-
Committee will be assisted to focus on monitoring the responsibilities of the Municipal Engineer for
construction and O&M, E/ while the Finance and Administration Sub-Committee will be assisted
with respect to the Fmancial Improvement (see Annex 5) and District Reorganization components of
the Project. Assistance will be provided by the three field based consultants (i.e., Engineering
Advisor, Finance Advisor, and Management Advisor; see Annex 15) and through participation by
Sub-Committee members in relevant training (see Annex 10).
14.   Management Team-Building. Although PNDC Law No. 207 decentralized 22 Centra
Government departments and asserted the principle that the staff of those departments assigned to
District levels would henceforth be responsible to the respective District Assemblies, tha has not
occurred in practice. District Chief Executives and District Administrative Officers have complained
that each of the decentralized departments continue to operate as separate and discrete entities, the
management and staff of which have not responded to the policy priorities or instructions of District
Assemblies. The result has been that managerial and technical resources officially available to the
District Assemblies have not been integrated into a cohesive organization sharing a common mission
and priority objectives. Indeed, the continual existence of parochial atachments by managers and
staff to their former ministries was a primary reason for the enactment of the Civil Service Law, 1993
and the drafting of a new Local Government Act for expected legislative approval during 1993.
15.   Thus, the objective of this component is to integrate various managers and supervisors
responsible to the 11 Project District Assemblies into an integrated District Management Team. The
primary mechanism for management team-building is the preparation of annual implementation action
plans by each of the 11 IDistrict Assemblies for all project components. That requirement will serve
the need for operationally feasible project implementation plans and indicators for monitoring those
plans. At the same time, the process employed for the preparation of those plans will serve to
develop the capacity to integrate the participation of managers and staff of the 11 new reorganized
District Departments.
16.   The following annual sequence of activities will serve as the catalyst for fostering integrated
management at the District level: (i) an Action-Planning workshop; (Hi) program planning; (iii)
C Cnddeat will be given in each of the 11 Project District Assemblies o tho establisme of a
separae Opera  and Maintnance Oversigiw Sub-Committee as part of the Distict Reorgaizaon

technical review; and (iv) District Mangement Team decision-meetings. An importat actvity wil
be the preparation of anmnal operational plans within the context of clear development strategies
(including both investnent and recurrent expenditure prections and commitments). Eventually, the
process will encompass both investment and recurrent operational planning for all of the programs
and organizaional entities within district government, but, during the initial tee year implementatlon
period of LGDP, these annual exercises will be limited to operational work-plans for Project
implementation and resulting O&M requirements. In addition, the first annual Action-Planning
Workshop will include consideration of the District reorganization agenda of the LGDP, thereby
laying the foundation for the detailed design of the reorganization to follow (paras. 10-12). The
Action-Planning Workshops and program focussed planning exercises will cross-cut sectoral and
organizational barriers, thus increasing the need for interaction among all district level staff.
17.  lTis program is not self-implementing; it cannot be expected to be successfully implemened
at the outset by itself. There are specialized techniques which will need to be employed to catalyze
the process until such time as it becomes routine and can be managed by the District Management
Team itself (i.e., 36 months). Therefore, LGDP wi}l finance four Ghanaian consultants with
organizational development process skills to assist the 11 District Management Teams (and the
District Planning Officer) to design, organize, and conduct the annual district development plamning
process (see Annex 15, Section 3). A total of 160 person-months of national consultants TA will be
provided for the management improvements discussed above.
18.   Personal Serices Contracts. Ardcle 17 of the Civil Service Law, 1993 provides for the
'contracting out of specific areas in the (public) Service to private persons." The Office of the Head
of the Civil Service (OHCS) has confirmed that one of the intentions of that provision, combined with
those enabling District Assemblies to exercise more control over staff employment, is to eventually
rely on District Assemblies to employ and finance their own staff and to foster, in that context, a
labor market approach to public sector employment. Such an approach to public sector employment
at the district-level, through the use of personal services contracts, was also recommended in the
Bank's recent decentralization sector report.
19.   A labor market approach to public employment at district levels responds to the demonstrated
problem that incentives for civil servants generally, and those serving in remote districts in particular,
do not adequately foster effective and efficient performance. Thus, private sector contracting and
personal services contracts allow District Assemblies, to the extent that financing is available, to craft
terms and conditions of service which compensate for the differential envronments presented by work
in one place rather than another. In that way, the market can be expected to determine the reasonable
cost of necessary incentives.
20.   As a complementary step towards rationalizing their internal organizaion, the I1 LGDP
District Assemblies will establish the positions of Municipal Engineer, Finance Officer, and Personel
Officer to head the Departments of Works and Finance and the Personnel Unit in the Department of
General Administration, respectively. Officers will be directly recruited for these positions from
outside the established Civil Service by the 11 District Assemblies themselves under the terms of
individually negotiated six-year fixed (renewable) Personal Services Contracts.
21.   The financing of the pilot personal services contract scheme will be based on the same
premise as the Project's O&M component; i.e., District Assemblies own-generated revenues will be
increased significantly as the Project sponsored Revenue Improvement Action Plans (RJAPs) are
implemented. Thus, District Assemblies will be expected to increase their own contribution to the

positions per District Assembly will be finced by Central Governnent for the first two years.
During the third and fourth year, Centra Govenment wffil finance 60 percent of the cost; District
Assemblies the other 40 perce. During the fift year, District Assemblies will finance 60 percent of
the cost; Central Government the remaining 40 percent. By the sixth year of the contract, the District
Assembly would finance 100 percent of the cost. The Figure below illustrates the financing
arrangement.

and Imlem on of on-the-job and classroom training programs and orientation workshops,
development of trang mawtls, and traning of trainers and workhop facilitators.
5.       iects of Trainin&Based on a preliminary survey of skill deficiencies among District

sta); (il) accutng and bookVeeping (for Revenue Inpectors); Civ) project plamnng (for District

6.    Types of Training. In assessing the human resource needs of the 11 District Assemblies
included within the project and the needs of MLGRD to coordinate support to the sector, LGDP will
finance a combination of on-the-job training, courses at Ghanaian training institutions, and external
seminars and workshops.
7.    On-e-Job Training(OllW . Targeted instruction will be provided at work sites on a periodic
basis. Topics will include: (i) work procedures, (i) equipment operation, (iii) efficient use of
materials, (iv) planning work assignments, (v) maintenance procedures, (vi) management of the work-
place, (vii) safety, and other related topics. Instruction will be provided primarily by senior staff to
their subordinates and by technical assistance personnel financed by the Project (see Annex 15).
Emphasis will be placed on standardizing procedures and developing a team approach to work
assignments. Depending upon the requirements of individual District Assemblies, these modules are
estimated to involve 2 to 4 hours of instruction per month on a continual basis throughout the year.
8.    Courses at Local Training Institutjpps. Numerous training programs are currently provided at
Government institutions. With respect to various types of planning and management training,
available institutions include the Ghana Institute of Management and Public Administration (GIMPA),
the Management Development and Productivity Institute (MDPI), and the School of Administaton of
the University of Ghana (UG/SA). Within the private sector, the Program for Effective Management
(PEM) and the Institute of Management and Administrative Sciences (IMAS) also offer planning and
management training courses. Various types of technical skills training is offered by the University
of Science and Technology (USI), the Polytechnics in Accra, Tamale, Takoradi, Ho and Kumasi, the
Accra Technical Training College, the Kumasi Technical Institute, and the technical training centers
of GWSC and MWH. The Accra Technical Training College and Kumasi Technical Institute have
been receiving considerable assistance from the Government of Saskatchewan through CIDA, which
maintains an Office of Technical Education in Accra for support of that program. To the extent
appropriate to the specific requirements of LGDP, courses normally provided by appropriate training
institutions will be relied on to improve job skills at both senior and junior levels. However, other
courses wfll need to be custom designed to respond to specific requests of District Assemblies.
9.    Orientation Training for District Assembly Councilors and Members of Parliament. Two
types of orientation courses will be provided to District Assembly Councilors and Members of
Parliament: (i) functions of District Assemblies and the roles of District Assembly Councilors and
District Officers provided by MLGRD and Q) alternative systems for the distribution of revenue
sources among central government and District Assemblies provided by consultants to Parliament's
Finance Committee andlor the Financial Management consultants (such training for District Assembly
Councilors would be limited primarily to sources of finance available to District Assemblies).
10.   Enal Seminas and WorkshA. During the first 5 year period of the LGDP, the Project
will finance participation of two senior persons from each of 11 DAs to attend seminars and
workshops outside Ghana. Seminars and workshops will be conducted by internationally recognized
institutions in the field of public administration and local government operations. Each District
Assembly will include proposed eternal seminars or workshops in their relevant annual training
plans. Witi respect to extnal workshops and seminars, information in the annual training plans will
include: (i) the trainee's name, tite, time in post and job fimctions, (ii) previous training received
during the past 3 to 5 years, (Hi) seminar/workshop topics and host institution, and (iv) costs
including tuition, travel and living expenses.

11.   PrenWaratio of Annual Training Plans. Each District Assembly will be assisted by field-based
management advisers (see Annexes 9 and 15) to prepare annual training plans specifying the mix of
training inputs proposed for its staff. These annual training plans will provide the basis for the timely
allocation of f inds, support materials, and external assistance during the subsequent year. Annual
training plans will include: (i) topics to b5 covered, (ii) names of participants, (iii) the insdtution
providing training, (iv) the timing and duration of the course, (v) the cost of tuition, (vi) training
materials required, (vii) cost of external training services (when used) and materials, and (viii)
scheduling. Plans will be approved by MLGRD or retuned to the respective District Assemblies
with recommendations for changes. The plans for orientation courses for District Assembly
Councilors and Members of Parliament will be prepared by MLGRD.
Implementation Responsibilities
12.   Each District will be responsible for preparing and managing its own individual annual
training program and will be provided with a specified annual training budget under the terms of the
Project. MLGRD will be responsible for coordinating any LGDP-related training which involves the
staff of more hn one District Assembly when such Assemblies request joint participation in training
courses. Ihe Ministry will also be responsible for managing specified orientation training for District
Assembly Councilors and Members of Parliament and for informing District Assemblies of training
opportundes within Ghana and abroad. The Technical Services Centre (ESC) will be responsible for
ensuring that activities financed under this sub-component are consistent with activities undertaken as
part of other project components; as well as overall financial management, monitoring, procurement,
accounting, disbursement, auditing, and reporting functions. Nevertheless, it is important to stress
here ta  most training financed by LGDP to staff and members of legislative assemblies at district
level will be demand-driven; i.e., responsive to the specific capacity needs of each District Assembly
as identified by senior district level staff and approved by the District Assembly itself. In response to
such initiatives by individual District Assemblies, consultants and tralning institutions will collaborate
to develop appropriate training materials and course content. This aspect of course development will
draw extensively from similar activities funded under the Curriculum Development component of the
Urban II Project.
Fstbmted Costs

cost, excluding finance of technical assistance not exclusively provided for the training sub-component
described here (see Annex 15 for costs of all project-related technical assistance).

REPUBLIC OF GHANA
LOCAL GOVERNMENT DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Implementation Arangements
1.    Implementation arrangements for LGDP will necessarily take Into account GOG's current
decentralization policy as embodied in various laws and regulations, both current and pending (See
Annex 9). In summary the premise of Ghana's decentralization policy is that District Assemblies will
have complete devolved responsibility for policy formulation, planning, implementation and
maintnce with respect to, among other things: (i) community development; (ii) town and country
planning; (iii) public works; and the construction, rehabilitation, and maintenance of (iv) roads and
streets; (v) markets; (vi) sanitation; (vii) water; (viii) electricity; and (ix) motor parks. To fulfill
those responsibilities, tender boards have been established at both District and Regional levels (the
former authorized to act with respect to procurement under approximately US$2 million; the latter
fom approximately US$2 million to $5 million).
2.    Nevertheless, District Assemblies remain almost completely dependent on Central
Govemnment for investment finance and for staff to implement, operate, and maintain such
investments. LGDP project implementation arrangements take that current reality into account, while
also recognizing that policies are evolving and district-level capacity is expected to improve.
Description
3.    Figure 1 summarizes the principles on the basis of which responsibilities have been assigned
among MLGRD, TSC and District Assemblies and the way that the implementation arrangements for
LGDP respond to those principles.
4.    Because of the existing capacity of TSC which has been built up through three previous IDA-
supported projects, and the support being provided to strengthen MLGRD under the Urban II project
curreny underway, 1/ primary reliance will be placed on those two Government entities to
implement LGDP. Nevertheless, because LGDP is the first IDA-financed urban development project
to be initated under the terms of Government's current decentralization policy, District Assemblies
will have significant responsibilities for implementing particular aspects of LGDP. A fuller
description of implementation arrangements follows.
VI    MLGRD is being assisted to (a) establish a Local Government Support Unit (LGSU) to assist,
coordinate, and monitor district Assemblies with respect to their roles in implementation of
urban inastucture projects and (b) improve its capacity to meet, at least in part, the trini
needs of District Assemblies by the establishment of a Training Support Unit MSU)
responsible for designing and implementing a Local Government Training Program.



-114-                                    Annex 11
Page 2 of 6

arrangements for each project component are fiuther explained in the various other Annexes. In
summary, MLAD wfll have overall responsibility for project implementation. In addition, MLGRD
will be directly responsible for the institudonal strengthening component, especially with respect to
procurement and supervision of technical assistance for improving local revenue collection
performance, financial management systems, and rationalization of the districts organizational
structure and staffing.
